المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في توحيده تعالى
الممكنات لاستلزامه أحد المحالين إما وقوع مقدور بين قادرين، وأما الترجيح بلا مرجح (الثاني) من الوجهين ( إذا أراد أحدهما شيئا فإما أن يمكن من الآخر إرادة ضده أو يمتنع وكلاهما محال، أما الأول فلانا نفرض وقوع إرادته له لأن الممكن لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال فيلزم إما وقوعهما معاً ،فيلزم اجتماع الضدين، وإما لا وقوعهما فيلزم ارتفاعهما فيلزم عجزهما لعدم حصول مرادهما وأيضاً يلزم اجتماعهما لأن المانع من وقوع مراد كل منهما هو حصول مراد الآخر لا قادريته عليه، فإذا امتنع مراد كل منهما فقد حصل مرادهما معاً هذا خلف ) وأيضاً فإذا فرض) ما ذكرناه ( في ضدين لا يرتفعان كحركة جسم وسكونه لزم المحال)، وهو ارتفاعهما معاً ( وأما وقوع أحدهما دون الآخر فالذي لا يقع مراده لا يكون قادراً كاملاً فلا يكون إلهاً .
قوله : ( لاستلزامه أحد المحالين رد عليه الأستاذ المحقق بأن وجود شيء من على تقدير تعدد إلا له لا يستلزم شيئان من المحالين لجواز أن يوجد بأحدهما التعلق إرادته واختياره دون إرادة الآخر والصواب في تقرير هذا الدليل أن يقال لو وجد إلهان لجاز أن يتعلق إرادتهما جميعا بمقدور معين، وحينئذ إن لم يوجد لزم عجزهما وإن وجد لزم أحد المحالين وقد يجاب بأن المراد بالقدرة في قوله قادران على الكمال القدرة المستجمعة بجميع شرائط التأثير ومن جملتها تعلق الإرادة فمعنى كلام الشارح لو تعدد الإله القادر المستجمع بشرائط التأثير لم يوجد شيء من الممكنات لاستلزامه أحد المحالين والتالي باطل بشهادة الحس بوجوده ولزوم العجز وفيه نظر إذ لا يكون حينئذ دليلاً على انتفاء تعدد الإله مطلقاً ومن البين أن المدعي ذلك سواء تعلق إرادتهما بالفعل بإيجاد شيء من الأشياء أو تعلق إرادة أحدهما دون الآخر. قوله : ( لأن الممكن لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال أي نظراً إلى ذاته، وأما لزومه نظراً إلى الامتناع الغير فلا يقدح في الاستدلال لتعين أن المحال فيما نحن فيه إنما يلزم من تعدد الإله فيمتنع وهو المطلوب .
قوله : ( فيلزم عجزهما فإن قلت المعتزلة يجوزون تخلف المراد عن الإرادة فيلزم أن لا يتم هذا الدليل عندهم مع أنه للمتكلمين قاطبة، قلت : المشيئة عندهم نوعان مشيئة قسر ومشيئة تفويض إلى اختيارهم فالعجز واقع في الأول، إذا لم يحصل المراد والكلام مسوق على ذلك ولك أن تقول هم إنما يجوزون التخلف في الفعل الاختياري لغيره لا في فعل نفسه، والبحث فيه . وأما قوله : كحركة جسم وسكونه فلا يقتضي فرضهما اختياريين لذلك الجسم وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( وأيضاً يلزم اجتماعهما لأن المانع إلخ فيه بحث لاحتمال أن يكون نفس تعلق إرادة أحدهما بالضد مانعاً من وقوع الضد الآخر لا بسبب استلزام حصول المراد حتى يرجع إلى ما ذكره الشارح من أن المانع من وقوع مراد كل منهما وقوع مراد الآخر بل بسبب لزوم الترجيح بلا مرجح، فليتأمل .
قوله : ( فالذي لا يقع مرادهلا يكون قادراً كاملاً إذ المفروض إمكان تعلق إرادته ووقوع ذلك الممكن فحديث جواز التفويض، وهم وأما منع لزوم انتفاء القدرة الكاملة بناء على أن تعلق الإرادة الأخرى بالطرف الآخر أخرجه أخرجه عن حد الإمكان فلا يكون عدم وقوعه منافيا لتحقق القدرة الكاملة، فهو أيضاً مدفوع بأن وقوع لما كان ممكناً في نفس الأمر وسد الغير طريق القدرة عليه لزم عجزه بتعجيز الغير إياه وهذا ظاهر جداً .
( وأما الثاني ) وهو أن يمتنع إرادة الآخر ضده ( فلان ذلك الشيء) الذي امتنع تعلق إرادة الآخر به هو ( لذاته يمكن تعلق قدرة كل من الإلهين وإرادته به فالذي امتنع تعلق قدرته ) وإرادته ) به فالمانع عنه هو تعلق قدرة الآخر ) وإرادته ( فيكون هذا عاجزاً ) فلا يكون إلهاً، هذا خلف ) لأنه خلاف المقدور. قوله : ( فالمانع عنه هو تعلق قدرة الآخر) فيكون هذا عاجزاً فإن قلت جاز أن يكون المانع المصلحة في الضد الآخر ، قلت : كون الإرادة تابعة للمصلحة ممنوع وأما مايقال بعد تسليمه من أنا نفرض الكلام في ضدين استوت وجوه المصلحة فيهما، ولا شك في إمكان ضدين كذلك غايته أنه لم يثبت وجودهما ولا ضير فإن الفرض يكفينا فقد يناقش فيه بأنه يحتمل أن يكون التخالف عند استواء المصالح فيهما من جملة المصالح، وإن أمكن أن يدفع عن أصل الدليل بفرض الإرادتين معاً فليتأمل. فإن ( قلت ) : لا نسلم لزوم العجز فإن تعلق قدرة أحدهما جعل تعلق قدرة الآخر ممتنعاً ولا عجز في مثله قلت قد عرفت مما سبق الآن اندفاعه وبهذا أيضاً يندفع منع لزوم عجزهما على تقدير عدم حصول مرادهما بناء على أن تعلق القدرتين أخرجهما عن حد الإمكان فليتدبر.
The third watch - first purpose: concerning His oneness, may He be exalted. The possible things [require] its necessitating one of two impossible [outcomes]: either the occurrence of an act of power (maqdur) between two powerful ones (qadirān), or preference without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). The second of the two aspects: if one of them wills a thing, then either the other is able to will its opposite or is unable; both are impossible. As for the first, we assume the occurrence of his will for it because the possible does not necessitate from assuming its occurrence an impossibility; then it necessitates either their occurrence together, which necessitates the conjunction of two opposites, or their non-occurrence, which necessitates their both being lifted, which necessitates their impotence due to the non-attainment of their wills. Also, it necessitates their conjunction because the preventer of the occurrence of the willed object of each is the attainment of the willed object of the other, not his capability over it. So if the willed object of each is prevented, then their wills have both been attained—this is contrary [to the assumption]. Also, if we assume what we mentioned regarding two opposites that cannot be lifted, like the motion of a body and its rest, the impossible is necessitated, which is their being together lifted. And as for the occurrence of one without the other, the one whose will does not occur is not completely powerful (qādiran kāmilan), so he is not a god. His statement: (For its necessitating one of two impossible [outcomes]) was objected to by the verifying master (al-ustādh al-muḥaqqiq) that the existence of anything on the assumption of multiplicity of gods does not necessitate any of the two impossible [outcomes] because it is possible that it exists by the attachment of the will and choice of one of them without the will of the other. And the correct way to present this proof is to say: if there were two gods, it would be possible that their wills both attach to a specific act of power (maqdūr muʿayyan). Then, if it does not exist, their impotence is necessitated; and if it exists, one of the two impossible [outcomes] is necessitated. It may be answered that what is meant by power in his statement 'two powerful ones' is the power that is complete, gathering all conditions of efficacy, among which is the attachment of will. So the meaning of the commentator's words: if the powerful god who gathers the conditions of efficacy were multiple, nothing of the possible things would exist because it necessitates one of the two impossible [outcomes], and the consequent is false by the testimony of sense perception of its existence and the necessity of impotence. And there is consideration in this, for then it would not be a proof for the negation of multiplicity of god absolutely, and it is clear that the claimant [denies multiplicity] whether their wills attach to the act by bringing into existence something or the will of one of them attaches without the other. His statement: (Because the possible does not necessitate from assuming its occurrence an impossibility) means with respect to its essence (li-dhātihi); as for its necessity with respect to external impossibility (al-imtināʿ al-ghayrī), it does not detract from the inference because it is determined that the impossibility in our case only follows from the multiplicity of god, so it becomes impossible, and that is the sought [conclusion]. His statement: (Then their impotence is necessitated). If you say: the Muʿtazila permit the non-occurrence of the willed object from the will, so this proof would not be established for them even though it is for all theologians (mutakallimūn). I say: the will (al-mashīʾa) according to them is of two types: a coercive will (mashīʾa qasr) and a will that delegates to their choice (mashīʾa tafwīḍ ilā ikhtiyārihim); so impotence occurs in the first type when the willed object is not attained, and the discourse is based on that. And you may say: they only permit non-occurrence in the voluntary act of another, not in his own act, and the investigation is about that. And as for his statement: 'like the motion of a body and its rest', it does not require assuming them both voluntary for that body, and that is apparent. His statement: (And also it necessitates their conjunction because the preventer... etc.) contains investigation because it is possible that the very attachment of the will of one to the opposite is a preventer of the occurrence of the other opposite, not due to the implication of the attainment of the willed object such that it returns to what the commentator mentioned that the preventer of the occurrence of the willed object of each is the occurrence of the willed object of the other, but due to the necessity of preference without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). So let it be pondered (falyataʾammal). His statement: (The one whose will does not occur is not completely powerful) since it is assumed possible the attachment of his will and the occurrence of that possible, so the talk of permissible delegation is an illusion. And as for the denial of the necessity of the negation of complete power based on the fact that the attachment of the other will to the other side took it out of the realm of possibility, so its non-occurrence is not contrary to the realization of complete power—this too is refuted because the occurrence, since it was possible in itself and another blocked the path of power over it, his impotence follows due to the other incapacitating him, and this is very apparent. (And as for the second) which is that the other is unable to will its opposite—because that thing whose attachment of the other's will to it is prevented is, in itself, possible for the attachment of the power and will of each of the two gods. So that whose attachment of his power and will to it is prevented, the preventer is the attachment of the power and will of the other, so this one is impotent, so he is not a god—this is a consequence contrary to the assumption. His statement: (The preventer is the attachment of the power of the other), so this one is impotent. If you say: it is possible that the preventer is the benefit (maṣlaḥa) in the other opposite. I say: that the will is subject to benefit is denied. And as for what is said after accepting it, that we assume discourse about two opposites whose aspects of benefit are equal, and there is no doubt about the possibility of two opposites being like that, the only issue is that their existence is not established, but the assumption suffices us. It may be objected that it is possible that disagreement when the benefits are equal is among the benefits themselves, although it is possible to defend the original proof by assuming both wills together. So let it be pondered. Then (if you say): we do not accept the necessity of impotence because the attachment of the power of one made the attachment of the power of the other impossible, and there is no impotence in such a case. I say: you have known from what preceded now its refutation, and by this also the denial of the necessity of their both being impotent on the assumption of non-occurrence of their wills is refuted, based on the fact that the attachment of the two powers removed them from the realm of possibility. So let it be reflected upon (falyatadabbar).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a dense rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) for the oneness of God (tawḥīd) attributed to the theologians (mutakallimūn). It begins by stating that if there were two fully powerful gods, then for any possible thing (mumkin), one of two impossible outcomes (muḥāl) must follow: either a single act would have two agents (which is impossible), or there would be preference without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), which is also impossible. The author then develops two cases. In the first case, both gods will the same thing. The proof argues that either the thing occurs (which would imply two independent causes for one effect, an impossibility), or it does not occur (which would mean both gods are impotent, contradicting their divinity). A similar argument applies to two opposites (like motion and rest): both cannot occur together, but if neither occurs, that also leads to impossibility. In the second case, one god wills something and the other is unable to will its opposite. This inability must be due to the other god's power, making the second god impotent. The commentary then addresses objections: (1) The Muʿtazila allow that will can be ineffective, but this is refuted by distinguishing types of will (coercive vs. delegated). (2) The objection that the preventer might be the other will itself rather than the outcome is examined and shown to lead to an infinite regress or vicious circle. (3) The possibility that the preventer is a benefit (maṣlaḥa) is denied because will is not necessarily subject to benefit. Finally, the objection that the attachment of one power makes the other power impossible (so no impotence) is refuted: if one power blocks the other, it implies the second is not truly all-powerful. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the proof stands and that the existence of two absolutely powerful gods is logically impossible.

Key Terms
مشيئة قسر
مشيئة تفويض
قادران
إرادة
محال
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the proof only works if one assumes both gods always will the same thing, but if they could coordinate or have different wills, the proof fails.
Correction: The proof covers both possibilities: both willing the same and one willing the opposite; both lead to contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does Al-Jurjani respond to the Mu'tazilite objection that will can be ineffective?
A) He denies the possibility entirely.
B) He distinguishes between coercive will and delegated will.
C) He concedes and modifies the proof to accommodate it.
D) He ignores the objection.

Key Takeaway
The objections against the proof of tawhid do not invalidate it; careful analysis shows the proof stands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson refutes objections that might arise in your own mind. Have you ever felt that the logical proofs for Allah's existence are weak because of such objections? After studying this, will you commit to not letting superficial doubts shake your certainty?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that will can be ineffective, which would undermine the proof. Do you ever act as if Allah's will can be frustrated by your plans or desires? Have you truly internalized that nothing happens without His complete power and will?
?
The proof stands on the impossibility of two omnipotent beings. In your daily life, do you ever act as if there are other 'gods'—such as wealth, status, or your own desires—that compete with Allah's authority? How will you actively reject these false sources of power?

The Argument from Incapacity for Divine Unity
Al-Jurjani presents a proof that mutual prevention of wills entails incapacity, refuting dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani uses the concept of incapacity to refute dualism and defend divine unity.

Core Idea
If two gods exist, one would be incapable, which contradicts divinity.

This passage presents a key rational argument against the existence of two gods. Al-Jurjani argues that if two gods had conflicting wills, the prevention of one by the other would entail incapacity, negating divinity. He also addresses objections to strengthen the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the logical steps and the objections.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the core logical step: the prevention of one god's will by the other leads to incapacity, which contradicts divinity.
Look for the phrase 'فيكون هذا عاجزاً فلا يكون إلهاً'.
3
Understand the objections
Note the two objections raised: (1) the preventer could be benefit, (2) the prevention does not entail incapacity. See how Al-Jurjani responds to each.
The responses rely on denying that will follows benefit and referring to a previous refutation.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important for Islamic theology: it upholds tawheed by rationally proving that only one God can exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو أن يمتنع إرادة الآخر ضده فلان ذلك الشيء الذي امتنع تعلق إرادة الآخر به هو لذاته يمكن تعلق قدرة كل من الإلهين وإرادته به فالذي امتنع تعلق قدرته وإرادته به فالمانع عنه هو تعلق قدرة الآخر وإرادته فيكون هذا عاجزاً فلا يكون إلهاً، هذا خلف لأنه خلاف المقدور وقد مر أنه يمكن إثبات الوحدانية بالدلائل النقلية لعدم توقف صحتها على التوحيد. قوله : ( فالمانع عنه هو تعلق قدرة الآخر) فيكون هذا عاجزاً فإن قلت جاز أن يكون المانع المصلحة في الضد الآخر ، قلت : كون الإرادة تابعة للمصلحة ممنوع وأما مايقال بعد تسليمه من أنا نفرض الكلام في ضدين استوت وجوه المصلحة فيهما، ولا شك في إمكان ضدين كذلك غايته أنه لم يثبت وجودهما ولا ضير فإن الفرض يكفينا فقد يناقش فيه بأنه يحتمل أن يكون التخالف عند استواء المصالح فيهما من جملة المصالح، وإن أمكن أن يدفع عن أصل الدليل بفرض الإرادتين معاً فليتأمل. فإن ( قلت ) : لا نسلم لزوم العجز فإن تعلق قدرة أحدهما جعل تعلق قدرة الآخر ممتنعاً ولا عجز في مثله قلت قد عرفت مما سبق الآن اندفاعه وبهذا أيضاً يندفع منع لزوم عجزهما على تقدير عدم حصول مرادهما بناء على أن تعلق القدرتين أخرجهما عن حد الإمكان فليتدبر.
As for the second [point], which is that the will of the other [god] being prevented from its opposite [is impossible because] that thing which the other will is prevented from attaching to is, in itself, such that the power and will of each of the two gods can attach to it. So that which is prevented from having his power and will attach to it – the preventer of that is the attachment of the other's power and will. Hence, this one is incapable, so he is not a god. This is a contradiction, because it contradicts what is presupposed. And it was mentioned earlier that it is possible to establish unity by transmitted proofs, since their validity does not depend on [prior proof of] unity. He says: (So the preventer of it is the attachment of the other's power) so this one is incapable. If you say: It is possible that the preventer is the benefit in the opposite [act], I say: That the will follows benefit is denied. As for what is said after granting it – that we suppose the discussion concerns two opposites whose aspects of benefit are equal, and there is no doubt about the possibility of two opposites like that, except that their existence is not proven, but that does not harm because the supposition suffices – it may be objected that the difference when the benefits are equal might itself be among the benefits. Even if it is possible to defend the original proof by supposing both wills together, let this be examined. If you say: We do not grant the necessary incapacity, because the attachment of the power of one makes the attachment of the power of the other impossible, and there is no incapacity in such a case. I say: You have already learned from what preceded that this is refuted. And by this also is refuted the denial of the necessity of their incapacity on the assumption that their intended objects are not attained, based on the claim that the attachment of the two powers removes them from the realm of possibility. So let this be pondered.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof against dualism, demonstrating that if there were two gods, one would necessarily be incapable, which contradicts divinity. The author first states the second argument: it is impossible for one god's will to be prevented from its opposite by the other god's will. The reason is that the object in question is possible for both gods to will and have power over. If one god's will is prevented, it is because the other's will attaches to it. This makes the prevented god incapable, so he is not divine. This is a contradiction to the assumption of two gods. The author then addresses an objection: perhaps the preventer is a benefit (maslaha) in the opposite act. He denies that will follows benefit. Even if one grants that, a case of equal benefit can be supposed, and the objection is that the difference itself might be a benefit. Still, the proof can be saved by supposing both wills together. Another objection: the prevention does not imply incapacity because the first god's power made the second's attachment impossible. The author responds that this has already been refuted, and the same refutation applies to the claim that both are non-incapable even if their objects are not attained.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
عجز
إله
وحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that prevention by another god does not entail incapacity because the preventing god's power simply makes the act impossible.
Correction: The argument shows that if an object is possible in itself for both, then prevention by the other's will indicates that the prevented god lacks the power to act, i.e., is incapable.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if one god's will prevents the other god's will from acting, what follows?
A) The prevented god is incapable.
B) Both gods are incapable.
C) The preventing god is evil.
D) The prevented god is not affected.

Key Takeaway
The argument from incapacity shows that two gods are impossible because one would be rendered incapable, negating divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a logical argument that two gods are impossible because one would be incapable. But consider your own life: when you fail to obey Allah, do you sometimes act as if there is another 'will' competing with His? What does this argument reveal about the absolute exclusivity of His will and power, and how should that change your response to your own weakness?
?
The argument hinges on the fact that a prevented god is no god at all. Ask yourself: In your heart, have you ever acknowledged a 'preventer' that limits Allah's ability to guide you—such as your own desires, sin, or circumstances? How does this proof force you to abandon that excuse and rely entirely on His omnipotence?
?
Al-Jurjani addresses the objection that prevention could be due to 'benefit' (maslaha) and dismisses it. Reflect: Have you ever justified disobedience by telling yourself that Allah's wisdom might permit what He has forbidden? Does this lesson expose that as a rational evasion? Commit now to submitting to Allah's command without making excuses about hidden benefits.

Refutation of the Dualist Argument on Good and Evil
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that one being cannot cause both good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the dualist claim that one being cannot cause both good and evil, using denial, linguistic analysis, and a powerful dilemma.

Core Idea
A being is judged good or evil by its actions and power, not by origin of acts.

Dualists argue that two separate deities are needed for good and evil. Al-Jurjani dismantles this by first denying the premise, then introducing a linguistic nuance, and finally presenting a dilemma that forces the dualist into a contradiction. This passage is a masterclass in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the dualist claim and the denial
The dualists claim a single being cannot be both good and evil (as a source of both types). Al-Jurjani first denies this outright.
The denial is 'منع قولهم'.
3
Understand the linguistic concession
He then allows that if 'good' and 'evil' are defined by predominance, they cannot combine. But this is not the dualist's necessary conclusion.
This shows he engages with alternative definitions but stays focused on the core.
4
Follow the main argument
After the denial, he presents a strong dilemma: if the good being can prevent evil but doesn't, he is evil; if he cannot, he is incapable.
Each option refutes the dualist's claim about the nature of the good being.
5
Examine the objection and reply
A possible objection: the good being might abstain from preventing evil for a wise reason. Al-Jurjani replies that then the creation of evil could also be wise, so it does not imply two beings.
This closes the loophole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع قولهم الواحد لا يكون خيراً شريراً بمعنى أنه يوجد خيراً كثيراً وشراً كثيراً، اللهم إلا أن يراد بالخير من يغلب خيره على شره، وبالشرير من يغلب شره على خيره كما ينبئ عنه ظاهر اللغة فلا يجتمعان حينئذ في واحد، لكنه غير ما لزم مما ذكر بل اللازم منه هو المعنى الذي أشرنا إليه فلا يفيد إبطاله أي إبطال ما ليس بلازم ثم بعد هذا المنع والتنزل عنه يقال لهم الخير إن قدر على دفع شر الشرير ولم يفعله فهو شرير وإن لم يقدر عليه فهو عاجز. قوله : ( ولم يفعله فهو شرير ) لأن كل واحد من التروك المتعلقة بدفع كل شر لذلك الشرير الكثير الشرشر في نفسه فإن قلت يجوز أن يكون ترك الخير دفع شر الشرير لاشتماله على حكمة . قلت : فحينئذ يجوز أن يكون إيجاد الشرور أيضاً، كذلك فلا يلزم من إيجادها كون الواحد خيراً وشريراً.
And the answer: we deny their statement that one being cannot be good and evil in the sense that He brings about much good and much evil. Unless by 'good' is meant one whose good overwhelms his evil, and by 'evil' one whose evil overwhelms his good, as is indicated by the apparent meaning of language; then they are not combined in one. But that is not the necessary consequence of what they mentioned; rather, the necessary consequence is the meaning we have pointed to. So refuting what is not a necessary consequence is of no use. Then, after this denial and descending from it, it is said to them: if the good being is able to repel the evil of the evil-doer and does not do it, then he is evil; and if he is not able to do it, then he is incapable. His statement: (And if he does not do it, then he is evil) because each act of abstaining from repelling every evil of that evil-doer is much evil in itself. If you say: It is possible that the good one's abstaining from repelling the evil of the evil-doer is due to its involving wisdom. I say: In that case, it is also possible that the creation of evils also involves wisdom, so the creation of them does not necessitate that one is both good and evil.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist argument that one being cannot be both the source of good and evil. The author begins by denying the premise that one cannot be both good and evil in the sense of bringing about much good and much evil. He then allows for a linguistic interpretation: 'good' means one whose good predominates, and 'evil' means one whose evil predominates. In that sense, they cannot combine in one being. But he argues that this is not the necessary consequence of the dualists' own claim; the necessary consequence is the meaning he indicated earlier (i.e., that one being brings about both types of acts). Thus, refuting a non-necessary consequence is irrelevant. He then descends from the denial and presents a stronger argument: if the good being is able to repel the evil of the evil-doer and does not, then he is evil; if he cannot, then he is incapable. The commentator explains that each act of abstaining from repelling evil is itself evil. An objection is raised: perhaps the abstention is wise. The reply is that then the creation of evils could also be wise, so it does not follow that the creator is both good and evil.

Key Terms

شر

عاجز
غالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the inability to prevent evil implies divine weakness; the argument turns this back on the dualist by showing it leads to the good being being evil or incapable.
Correction: The text shows that if the good being is unable to prevent evil, he is 'عاجز' (incapable), which is a flaw. But the dualist faces a dilemma: either the good being is evil or incapable.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the refutation against the dualist claim that one being cannot be both good and evil?
A) They deny the premise outright.
B) They argue that if the good god can prevent evil and does not, he is evil.
C) They say both gods are evil.
D) They claim evil does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani refutes dualism by showing that a single being can be both good and evil without contradiction, using a dilemma that exposes the incoherence of the dualist position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil in the world, does it ever cause you to question whether the One who created everything is truly all-good and all-powerful? This lesson argues that the very question leads to a dead end if we assume separate creators. Where in your heart do you struggle to reconcile God's goodness with the existence of evil? Take a moment to write down that struggle and then ask yourself: am I willing to submit to the mystery of God's wisdom, trusting that He is both good and powerful even when I cannot see it?
?
The dualist claims that a good being would stop all evil if He could; therefore, if He doesn't, He must be evil or weak. This lesson turns that judgment back on the one making it. Think of a time when you judged God or others for allowing harm to happen. What does this lesson reveal about the limits of human judgment? Can you repent of placing yourself in the position of the questioner and instead embrace the role of the servant who trusts?
?
Al-Jurjānī says that even the abstention from preventing evil could be wise. This means that there is a purpose behind what God permits, even if it looks like evil to us. Is there an area of your life where you are angry with God for allowing something painful? What would change if you truly believed that His wisdom encompasses that event? Commit today to praying for the grace to trust His plan rather than demanding answers.

5 min
Refutation of the Dualist Claim about Good and Evil
The commentator refutes dualism by denying their claim and posing a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the dualist argument that one being cannot be both good and evil, using linguistic clarification and a conditional dilemma about divine power.

Core Idea
The dualist argument collapses into either a linguistic triviality or a logical dilemma that undermines God's perfection.

The dualists argued that one being cannot be the source of both good and evil, implying two separate deities—one good, one evil. The commentator refutes this by first clarifying that language allows calling someone 'good' if his good predominates, but that is not the dualists' intended meaning. Then he poses a dilemma: if the good being can prevent evil but does not, he is evil; if he cannot, he is powerless. Both are impossible for God, thus refuting dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Note the two distinct parts: a denial with a linguistic nuance, followed by a dilemma.
2
Understand the linguistic clarification
The commentator first denies the dualist claim in its literal sense (that one being cannot produce much good and much evil). Then he allows that if 'good' means one whose good overcomes evil, and 'evil' means one whose evil overcomes good, then they cannot combine—but this is not what the dualists meant.
This shows the dualist claim is either false (first sense) or irrelevant (second sense).
3
Grasp the conditional dilemma
After denying and conceding, the commentator poses a dilemma: If the good being (God) is able to prevent the evil being's evil but does not, then He is evil. If He is not able, then He is impotent. Both outcomes are impossible for God.
This refutes dualism by showing that their assumption leads to a contradiction with God's attributes.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Combine both steps: first, the dualist claim is ambiguous and either false or irrelevant; second, if we accept a strong sense of the claim, it forces an impossible characterization of God. Thus, dualism is defeated.
This is a classic refutation in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع قولهم (الواحد لا يكون خيراً شريراً) بمعنى أنه يوجد خيراً كثيراً وشراً كثيراً، (اللهم إلا أن يراد بالخير من يغلب خيره) على شره، (وبالشرير من يغلب شره) على خيره) كما ينبئ عنه ظاهر (اللغة) فلا يجتمعان حينئذ في واحد، (لكنه غير ما لزم) مما ذكر بل اللازم منه هو المعنى الذي أشرنا إليه (فلا يفيد إبطاله) أي إبطال ما ليس بلازم (ثم بعد هذا المنع والتنزل عنه يقال لهم الخير إن قدر على دفع شر الشرير ولم يفعله فهو شرير وإن لم يقدر عليه فهو عاجز)
And the answer is: we deny their statement that the one cannot be good (khayr) and evil (sharr), meaning that He produces much good and much evil. However, unless what is meant by 'good' is one whose good overcomes his evil, and by 'evil' one whose evil overcomes his good, as the apparent meaning of language indicates, then they do not combine in one. But that is not what is entailed by what they mentioned; rather what is entailed is the meaning we indicated, so refuting it (i.e., refuting what is not entailed) is useless. Then after this denial and concession, it is said to them: If the good (al-khayr) is able to prevent the evil of the evil one and does not do it, then he is evil; and if he is not able to prevent it, then he is impotent.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents the commentator's refutation of the dualist argument that one being cannot be both good and evil. The commentator begins by denying their claim in the sense that they mean one being cannot produce both much good and much evil. He then allows for a possible interpretation: if by 'good' they mean one whose good outweighs his evil, and by 'evil' one whose evil outweighs his good, then indeed they cannot combine in one person. However, this interpretation is not what the dualists actually intended; what they intended is the sense we referred to (that one being cannot be the source of both good and evil in a significant way). Therefore, refuting that unintended meaning is pointless. After this denial and concession, the commentator poses a dilemma: If the good being (God) is capable of preventing the evil that the evil being causes, and He does not do so, then He is evil (for not stopping evil). If He is not capable of preventing it, then He is impotent. This refutes the dualist position by showing that either way, the good being is either evil or impotent, both of which are impossible for God.

Key Terms
خير
شر
منع
لازم
عاجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the dualists' claim is that one being cannot perform both good and evil actions, and the refutation simply says God only does good.
Correction: The commentator actually shows that the dualist claim is ambiguous. In one sense it is false; in another sense it is irrelevant. Then he demonstrates that even if we accept their position, it leads to an impossible characterization of God as either evil or impotent, which destroys their argument.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator refute the dualist claim that one being cannot be both good and evil?
A) By proving that good and evil are identical in essence.
B) By showing that linguistic nuances allow the title 'good' or 'evil' based on dominance, and then posing a dilemma about power to prevent evil.
C) By asserting that God creates only good and not evil.
D) By denying the existence of evil altogether.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of dualism via linguistic clarification and a logical dilemma shows that the dualist position either relies on an irrelevant meaning or leads to an impossible view of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil in your own life, are you tempted to think that God is either uncaring or powerless? How does the dilemma in this lesson force you to abandon that excuse and instead trust in a wisdom you cannot fully see? What will you do the next time that temptation arises?
?
The dualist tries to blame evil on a separate source. In your personal struggles—like anger, greed, or pride—do you ever act as if there is a 'second god' inside you that you cannot overcome? How does the lesson's insistence that all power belongs to the one God challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions and seek His help?
?
If God is the sole creator of all that happens, including what we call evil, does that unsettle you? This lesson refutes a simplistic split. How can you deepen your trust in God's perfect goodness and power even when you cannot reconcile the existence of evil with your understanding of His character? What concrete step can you take this week to submit to His decree without reservation?

The Objection on Divine Power and the Distinction of Evil
Objection on impotence and response distinguishing evil's source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a being unable to prevent evil is impotent, and how the response distinguishes between evil occurring by choice versus by necessitation.

Core Idea
Possibility of repulsion suffices for impotence, even if evil is necessitated.

The passage begins with an objection against divine power: if God cannot prevent evil, He is impotent. The response clarifies that impotence only applies when evil arises from free choice and the agent cannot prevent it; however, when evil occurs through necessitation, the inability does not indicate imperfection. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the key principle that the mere possibility of repulsion is enough to establish impotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage carefully. Notice how the author quotes the opponent's claim and then qualifies it.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Find where the author distinguishes evil by choice from evil by necessitation. Understand why this distinction matters.
3
Connect to the earlier principle
The author refers to a prior principle: the possibility of repulsion suffices for impotence. Reflect on why this holds even with external necessitation.
4
Reflect on the defense of divine power
Consider how this argument defends divine perfection without attributing evil to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في توحيده تعالى
الممكنات فلا يصلح إلهاً فلا يوجد إلهان كما ذكرتم ( فنعارض خطابتهم بخطابة أحسن من ذلك مالاً وأكثر إقناعاً .
قوله : ( وإن لم يقدر عليه فهو عاجز قيل إنما يلزم العجز إذا استند الشرور إلى الشرير بالاختيار كما يشير إليه قولهم في النور أنه حي عالم ،قادر، وأما إذا استندت إليه بالإيجاب فلا وقد عرفت مما سبق أن إمكان الاندفاع في نفس الأمر يكفي في لزوم العجز ولا ينافيه الوجوب بالغير. قوله : ( فتعارض خطابتهم بخطابة أحسن من ذلك مآلا ليس المراد بخطابتهم ما يفيد الظن كيف وشبهتهم لا تفيد الشك، فضلا عن الظن بل ما لا يفيد اليقين وأما إطلاق الخطابة على مالاً دليلنا فللتشاكل، ثم أن في العبارة مناقشة وهيأن صيغة التفضيل يدل على حسن خطابتهم أيضاً، ولا يخفى بطلانه كيف ومآله الخلود في النار أعاذنا الله تعالى منه والجواب ما حققناه في حواشي المطول وحاصله أن أفعل التفضيل قد يقصد به تجاوز صاحبه وتباعده عن الغير في الفعل لا بمعنى تفضيله بالنسبة إليه بعد الاشتراك في أصل الفعل بل بمعنى أن صاحبه متباعد في أصل الفعل متزائد إلى كماله فالمعنى هاهنا بخطابة متباعدة بحسب الحسن من خطابتهم القبيحة متزائداً إلى كماله .
The third observation - the first purpose: On His Oneness - Exalted be He. The contingent things are not fit to be a god, so there are not two gods as you mentioned. So we counter their rhetoric with a rhetoric better than that in outcome and more convincing. His statement: 'And if he is not able over it, then he is impotent.' It is said that impotence only follows if evils are attributed to the evil one by choice, as their statement about light indicates that it is living, knowing, powerful; but if they are attributed to him by necessitation, then no. And you have known from what preceded that the possibility of repulsion in actual fact suffices for the occurrence of impotence, and necessitation by another does not contradict it. His statement: 'So they counter their rhetoric with a rhetoric better than that in outcome.' By 'their rhetoric' is not meant what yields conjecture — how could it, when their doubt does not yield doubt, let alone conjecture — but rather what does not yield certainty. As for applying 'rhetoric' to our proof, it is for parallelism. Then in the expression there is a criticism: that the superlative form indicates the goodness of their rhetoric as well, and its falsehood is not hidden — how so, when its outcome is eternal fire, may Allah protect us from it. The answer is what we verified in the glosses on al-Mutawwal, and its gist: that the superlative may be intended for the surpassing of its subject and his distancing from the other in the action, not in the sense of preferring him relative to it after sharing in the origin of the action, but in the sense that its subject is distant in the origin of the action, increasing towards its perfection. So the meaning here is: with a rhetoric distanced in terms of goodness from their ugly rhetoric, increasing towards its perfection.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence from the third observation (al-marsad al-thalith) on divine oneness. The author first quotes an opponent's claim that if a being cannot prevent evil, it is impotent. The reply distinguishes between evil arising from free choice (which would imply impotence if unable) versus by necessitation. The preceding discussion established that the mere possibility of repulsion is enough for impotence, even if the evil occurs through external necessitation. Then the author turns to a rhetorical counter-argument: the opponent's rhetoric is met with 'better rhetoric'. A critic objects that calling our proof 'rhetoric' and using a superlative ('better') concedes some goodness to the opponent's argument, whose outcome is hellfire. The answer, drawn from glosses on al-Mutawwal, explains that the superlative here does not imply shared goodness; rather, it indicates distance and surpassing—our rhetoric is completely removed from theirs in goodness and increases toward perfection. This preserves the absolute evil of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
العجز
الاختيار
الإيجاب
إمكان الاندفاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a being cannot prevent evil, it is automatically impotent regardless of the origin of the evil.
Correction: The author clarifies that impotence depends on whether the evil is chosen freely. If evil is necessitated, inability to prevent does not indicate imperfection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does inability to prevent evil indicate impotence?
A) When the evil is caused by an external force.
B) When the evil is chosen by the agent and he cannot prevent it.
C) Whenever the agent cannot prevent evil, regardless of cause.
D) Only if the agent is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
Impotence is only implied when an agent cannot prevent evil that it freely chooses, not when evil is necessitated by another.

?
The passage teaches that the mere possibility of repelling evil is enough to establish impotence if the agent could have done so. In your own life, is there a specific sin or harmful pattern you know you could change but haven't? Are you content to call yourself 'impotent' in that area? This week, commit to one concrete step to actually repel that evil, seeking God's strength to overcome your weakness.

The Rhetorical Counter and the Superficial Criticism
Rhetorical counter and criticism of the superlative form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical strategy of countering the opponent's argument with a 'better' rhetoric, and the initial criticism that using the superlative 'better' seems to concede some goodness to the opponent's rhetoric.

Core Idea
Calling our proof 'better' does not admit any goodness in the opponent's rhetoric.

The author introduces a rhetorical counter: we respond to the opponent's rhetoric with a rhetoric that is 'better' in outcome and more convincing. However, a critic points out a problem: by using the superlative form 'better', we seem to acknowledge that their rhetoric has some goodness. This lesson examines that criticism and sets the stage for the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the passage where the author says we counter their rhetoric with better rhetoric. Understand the context: the opponent's argument is based on doubt, not certainty.
2
Notice the criticism
Identify the criticism: the superlative 'better' seems to imply that the opponent's rhetoric also has some degree of goodness.
3
Recognize the seriousness
Reflect on why this criticism is serious: the opponent's argument leads to eternal fire, so it cannot have any goodness.
4
Prepare for the answer
Consider that the next lesson will resolve this tension by explaining the true meaning of the superlative.

The True Meaning of the Superlative Form
Answer explaining the superlative's meaning as distance and surpassing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's resolution: the superlative here indicates distance and surpassing, not shared participation in the quality.

Core Idea
The superlative 'better' means our rhetoric is entirely distanced from theirs, not that both have some goodness.

After raising the criticism that the superlative 'better' seems to admit goodness in the opponent's rhetoric, the author provides an answer based on a distinction from the glosses on al-Mutawwal. The answer reveals that the superlative can be used to express that the subject is far removed from the object in the quality, increasing toward perfection. Thus, our rhetoric is completely different in kind—it is good while theirs is evil, and 'better' emphasizes the absolute distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer carefully
Read the passage that provides the answer. Understand the technical distinction about the superlative.
2
Grasp the two meanings
Identify the two possible meanings of the superlative: one implying shared quality (normal comparison), the other implying distance and surpassing without sharing.
3
Apply the second meaning
Apply the second meaning to the context: our rhetoric is 'better' in the sense that it is entirely removed from their evil rhetoric and increasing in goodness.
4
See the resolution
Notice how this resolves the criticism without conceding any goodness to the opponent.

Proof of Divine Power from the Absurdity of Necessary Causation
Al-Jurjani presents the proof that if God were not Qadir, four absurdities follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God is Qadir (powerful) by showing that the alternative—being a necessary cause—leads to four impossible consequences.

Core Idea
If God were a necessary cause, four absurdities follow; their impossibility proves He is powerful.

Al-Jurjani presents a devastating logical argument that God must be Qadir (powerful) rather than a mere necessary cause. The argument shows that if God were a necessary cause, one of four absurdities would necessarily occur. Since all four are impossible, the assumption that God is not Qadir is false. This lesson walks through the proof step by step, clarifying each absurdity and the logical chain that forces the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read its literal translation. Let the logical structure sink in before we dissect it.
Notice the compact style: key terms like 'موجب بالذات' (necessary cause by essence) and 'تخلف الأثر' (posteriority of effect).
2
Identify the four absurdities
The passage enumerates four impossible consequences that would follow if God were a necessary cause. Find each one and understand why each is impossible.
The fourth absurdity is the most subtle: a perfect necessary cause cannot have its effect delayed.
3
Follow the implication chain
The proof then shows how these four absurdities are the only possibilities under the assumption of necessary causation. Trace the logical branching carefully.
Use the guided breakdown below to see the steps clearly.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since all four consequences are false, the original assumption (necessary cause) must be false. Therefore, God is Qadir—powerful and free in action.
This proof establishes divine power as a rational necessity, not just a revealed doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المصنف إلى الاحتجاج على كونه قادراً بقوله ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن قادراً بل موجباً بالذات (لزم أحد الأمور الأربعة إما نفي الحادث) بالكلية (أو عدم استناده إلى المؤثر أو التسلسل أو تخلف الأثر عن المؤثر) الموجب التام، (وبطلان) هذه (اللوازم) كلها (دليل بطلان الملزوم)، أما (بيان الملازمة) فهو (أنه على تقدير كونه تعالى موجباً إما أن لا يوجد حادث أو يوجد فإن لم يوجد فهو الأمر الأول وإن وجد فأما أن لا يستند) ذلك الحادث الموجود (إلى مؤثر) موجد (أو يستند فإن لم يستند فهو الثاني) من تلك الأمور (وإن استند فأما أن لا ينتهي إلى قديم أو ينتهي فإن لم ينته فهو الثالث) منها؛ لأنه إذا استند إلى مؤثر لا يكون قديماً ولا منتهيا إليه فلا بد هناك من مؤثرات حادثة غير متناهية مع كونها مترتبة مجتمعة وهو تسلسل محال اتفاقا (وإن انتهى فلا بد) هناك (من قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة من الحوادث) دفعاً للتسلسل (في الحوادث سواء كانت مجتمعة أو متعاقبة، (فيلزم الرابع) وهو الموجب التام ضرورة تخلف ذلك الحادث الصادر بلا واسطة عن القديم الذي يوجبه
And the author pointed to the argument for His being Qadir by His saying: 'Otherwise'—i.e., if He were not Qadir but rather a necessary cause by essence—'one of four things would be necessary: either the negation of the temporal event altogether, or its lack of dependence on an effect, or an infinite regress, or the posteriority of the effect to the perfect necessary cause. And the falsity of all these consequences is a proof of the falsity of the antecedent. As for the demonstration of the implication, it is that, on the supposition of His being a necessary cause, either a temporal event does not exist or it does exist. If it does not exist, that is the first matter. If it exists, then either it does not depend on an effect or it does. If it does not depend, that is the second. If it does depend, then either it does not terminate in an eternal or it does terminate. If it does not terminate, that is the third; because if it depends on an effect that is not eternal nor terminating to an eternal, then there must be an infinite series of temporal effects, being arranged and simultaneous—this is an infinite regress, impossible by consensus. And if it terminates, then there must be an eternal that causes a temporal event without an intermediate of temporal events, to avoid regress in temporal events whether simultaneous or successive. Thus the fourth follows, which is the perfect necessary cause necessarily implying the posteriority of that temporal event issued without an intermediate from the eternal that causes it.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the central proof that God must be Qadir (powerful) rather than a necessary cause (mujib bi al-dhat). The author (al-Iji) is quoted saying 'Otherwise'—meaning if God were not Qadir—then four absurdities would follow: 1) No temporal event exists at all; 2) A temporal event exists without any cause; 3) An infinite regress of causes; or 4) The effect is delayed after its perfect necessary cause. All four are false, so the assumption that God is not Qadir is false. The proof then systematically shows the implication: given a necessary cause, either a temporal event exists or not. If not, that is the first absurdity. If it exists, either it has no cause (second absurdity) or it has a cause. If it has a cause, either the chain of causes does not terminate in an eternal (infinite regress, third absurdity) or it does terminate. If it terminates, then there must be an eternal that directly produces a temporal event without any intermediate temporal cause. But then that eternal would be a perfect necessary cause, and the effect (the temporal event) would be delayed after the cause—which is impossible because a perfect necessary cause cannot have its effect delayed. Thus the fourth absurdity follows. All four are impossible, so the original assumption that God is a necessary cause is false, proving He is Qadir.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth absurdity is a logical contradiction (the effect cannot be later than the cause).
Correction: It is not a logical contradiction but an impossibility in terms of divine perfection: a perfect necessary cause cannot have its effect delayed because that would imply a change or deficiency in the cause.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth absurdity that follows from supposing God is a necessary cause?
A) No temporal event exists.
B) A temporal event exists without any cause.
C) Infinite regress of causes.
D) The effect is delayed after the perfect necessary cause.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of these four absurdities proves that God cannot be a necessary cause; rather, He is Qadir—powerful and free in His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that God is not a mere necessary cause but a powerful agent who acts freely. Do you live as if God's actions are necessary and predictable, or do you truly expect Him to intervene in your life in ways that break the 'natural order'? How would your dua change if you were certain of His absolute power over all causes?
?
If the fourth absurdity (delay of effect) is impossible for a perfect necessary cause, what does that say about your patience when God delays answering your prayers? Are you willing to trust that His delay is a sign of His free will and wisdom, not a limitation of His power?
?
The argument avoids absurdities that arise if God were not Qadir. Reflect on a time you acted as if God's hands were tied by secondary causes—like believing a doctor alone heals, or that your provision depends only on your work. How can this proof reshape your reliance on God alone?

The Four Impossibilities of Necessitarianism
Al-Iji presents the four impossibilities and their logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof for God's power by demonstrating four absurd consequences of necessitarianism.

Core Idea
If God were not powerful, four impossible things must follow.

Al-Iji begins by laying out the alternatives that would follow if God were a mere necessitarian (mujib bi-dhat). This logical division narrows down to four impossible outcomes, forming the backbone of the proof. The passage explains the entailment (mulazama) between the assumption of necessitarianism and these four consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's logical division come through.
Notice the conditional structure: if...then...
2
Identify the four consequences
The passage enumerates four impossibilities that would follow if God were not powerful but a necessitarian. Find each one in the text.
They are: (1) no originated things at all, (2) originated things without a cause, (3) infinite regress, (4) delayed effect.
3
Trace the logical division
Follow the binary choices in the text: either no originated thing exists or it does; if it does, either it has no cause or has a cause; if it has a cause, either the chain is infinite or terminates. This division leads to the four consequences.
This is a classic reductio structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا) أي وإن لم يكن قادراً بل موجباً بالذات (لزم أحد الأمور الأربعة إما نفي الحادث) بالكلية ( أو عدم استناده إلى المؤثر أو التسلسل أو تخلف الأثر عن المؤثر) الموجب التام ، ( وبطلان ) هذه (اللوازم ) كلها ( دليل بطلان الملزوم)، أما ( بيان الملازمة ) فهو ( أنه ( على تقدير كونه تعالى موجباً (إما أن لا يوجد حادث أو يوجد فإن لم يوجد فهو الأمر الأول وإن وجد فأما أن لا يستند ) ذلك الحادث الموجود ) إلى (مؤثر) موجد ( أو يستند فإن لم يستند فهو الثاني ) من تلك الأمور ( وإن استند فأما أن لا ينتهي إلى قديم أو ينتهي فإن لم ينته فهو الثالث ) منها؛ لأنه إذا استند إلى مؤثر لا يكون قديماً ولا منتهيا إليه فلا بد هناك من مؤثرات حادثة غير متناهية مع كونها مترتبة مجتمعة وهو تسلسل محال اتفاقا ( وإن انتهى فلا بد ) هناك ( من قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة من الحوادث ( دفعاً للتسلسل ) في الحوادث سواء كانت مجتمعة أو متعاقبة ، ( فيلزم الرابع ) وهو الموجب التام ضرورة تخلف ذلك الحادث الصادر بلا واسطة عن القديم الذي يوجبه ( وأما بطلان اللوازم فالأول بالضرورة والثاني بما علمت من أن الممكن الحادث محتاج إلى مؤثر، والثالث بما مر في مباحث التسلسل، والرابع بأن الموجب التام ما يلزمه أثره وتخلف اللازم عن الملزوم محال وبأنه يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح من فاعل موجب، فإن وجود ذلك الحادث منه في وقته ليس أولى من وجوده فيما قبله قيل هذا الدليل برهان بديع لا يحتاج إلى إثبات حدوث العالم وقد تفردبه المصنف الله رحمه تعالى
((Otherwise)) i.e., if He were not powerful but rather a necessitarian by essence, ((then one of four things would necessarily follow: either the negation of the originated (thing)) completely, ((or its not being based on an effect, or infinite regress, or the delay of the effect from the effect)) the complete necessitarian. ((And the falsity)) of all these ((consequences is proof of the falsity of the entailed)). As for ((the explanation of the entailment)) it is ((that on the assumption that He, exalted, is a necessitarian, either an originated thing does not exist or it exists. If it does not exist, that is the first matter; if it exists, then either it is not based)) on an efficient cause or it is based. If it is not based, that is the second of those matters; if it is based, then either it terminates at an eternal or it does not. If it does not terminate, that is the third of them; because if it is based on an efficient cause that is not eternal nor terminating at it, then there must be originated efficient causes without end, while they are arranged and simultaneous, and that is an infinite regress agreed to be impossible. And if it terminates, then there must be an eternal that necessitates an originated thing without any intermediary of originated things, to avoid infinite regress in originated things, whether they are simultaneous or successive. Then the fourth necessarily follows, i.e., the complete necessitarian, by necessity of the delay of that originated thing that came without intermediary from the eternal that necessitates it. And as for the falsity of the consequences: the first is by necessity; the second by what you know, that the originated possible needs an efficient cause; the third by what has passed in the investigations of infinite regress; and the fourth because the complete necessitarian is that whose effect necessarily follows it, and the delay of the necessary from the necessitating is impossible, and because it would entail preponderance without a preponderant from a necessitating agent, for the existence of that originated thing from Him at its time is not more likely than its existence before it. It is said: This proof is an excellent demonstration that does not need to establish the temporality of the world, and the author, may God have mercy on him, has uniquely presented it.)
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's power (qudra) by reductio ad absurdum against the necessitarian view (wujub bi-dhat). The argument: If God were not powerful but rather a necessitarian (i.e., His essence necessarily produces effects without will), then one of four impossible things must follow: (1) No originated things (hawadith) exist at all—but they do exist. (2) Originated things exist without a cause—but every originated possible needs a cause. (3) An infinite regress of causes (tasalsul)—already proven impossible. (4) The effect of a complete cause is delayed—impossible because the complete cause necessitates its effect immediately, and delay would require a preponderance without preponderant. The author explains the entailment (mulazama) by logical division: either no originated thing, or originated with/without cause, and if with cause, whether the chain ends or not. If it ends, the final cause must be eternal and produce an effect without intermediary, leading to the fourth impossibility. Each consequent is proven false: the first is self-evidently false; the second contradicts the need for a cause; the third refuted earlier; the fourth is incoherent. The proof is praised as a unique, brilliant demonstration that does not require proving the world's creation (huduth).

A) Negation of all originated things.
B) Infinite regress.
C) Delay of the effect.
D) Its not being based on an efficient cause.

Key Takeaway
If God were not powerful but a necessitarian, one of four impossible consequences must follow, which is the foundation of the proof for God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every event in your life is directly caused by God's power and will, why do you still worry about outcomes? How would your daily anxiety change if you lived with the certainty that nothing happens without His free choice, and that delay is impossible for Him?
?
The fourth impossibility shows that if God were a necessitarian, your prayers would be futile—He would have to act without your request. Since you know He is powerful and responds, what does that demand of you in your next supplication? Will you pray with the conviction that He can truly change your situation, or do you pray out of habit?
?
This proof eliminates chance and uncaused events completely. If you ever live as if your actions are independent of God's sustaining power, how can you repent from that assumption today? What step will you take to acknowledge His absolute control over your next breath?

Why Each Consequence Fails
Al-Iji refutes each of the four consequences of necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why each of the four consequences of necessitarianism is false, especially the impossibility of a delayed effect from a complete cause.

Core Idea
Each impossibility is demonstrably false, proving God must be powerful.

Having established what would have to follow, Al-Iji now demonstrates that each of those four consequences is false. The falsity of the fourth—the delayed effect—is especially important and is argued in two ways: the nature of a complete necessary cause and the problem of preponderance without a preponderant. The proof is praised as not requiring the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the falsity section
Read the Arabic passage about the falsity of the four consequences. Notice the concise references for the first three and the detailed argument for the fourth.
Each refutation is short but strong.
2
Understand the refutation of the fourth
Focus on the fourth: why can't the effect be delayed from a complete cause? Two reasons: (1) a complete cause necessitates its effect immediately, so delay is impossible; (2) delay would mean preponderance without a preponderant, because the cause would be equally capable before and after.
This is a key argument in Islamic theology for God's free will.
3
Appreciate the uniqueness of the proof
The author notes that this proof does not depend on proving the world's temporality (huduth), which is a unique advantage. Reflect on why that is significant.
It makes the proof independent of a controversial premise.

A Simplified Proof for God's Power
Al-Iji presents a concise proof against necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji proves God's power by showing the absurdity of a necessitarian view through the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
A necessitarian God would force an eternal world, but the world is originated.

Al-Iji offers a crisp argument to demonstrate that God must be powerful (qadir) rather than a mere necessitarian. The proof hinges on the impossibility of an infinite regress of originated conditions. This short passage is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the compact style: the proof is presented in a few lines.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if-then' structure: if God is a necessitarian, then the originated would be eternal. Highlight the consequence.
The premise is 'لو كان الباري تعالى موجباً بالذات لزم قدم الحادث'.
3
Trace the entailment via infinite regress
Follow the explanation: why must the effect be eternal? Because an originated effect would require an originated condition, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
This step shows the logical necessity behind the entailment.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the world is observed to be originated, the assumption of necessitarianism is false, proving that God is powerful (qadir) and acts with will.
This is the ultimate point: God's power is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شئت قلت : ) في إثبات كونه قادراً ( لو كان الباري تعالى موجباً بالذات لزم قدم الحادث والتالي باطل) بطلاناً ظاهراً ، (و) أما (بيان الملازمة ) فهو أن أثر الموجب القديم يجب أن يكون قديماً، إذ ( لو حدث لتوقف على شرط حادث) كيلا يلزم التخلف عن الموجب التام وذلك الشرط الحادث يتوقف أيضاً على شرط آخر حادث (و) حينئذ ( تسلسل ) أي لزم التسلسل في الشروط الحادثة متعاقبة أو مجتمعة وكلاهما محال .
((And if you wish, you may say:)) in establishing that He is powerful: ((If the Exalted Creator were a necessitarian by essence, then the eternality of the originated would follow, and the consequent is false)) obviously. ((And as for the explanation of the entailment)) it is that the effect of the eternal necessitarian must be eternal, because ((if it were originated, it would depend on an originated condition)) to avoid the delay from the complete necessitarian; and that originated condition also depends on another originated condition ((and)) then ((infinite regress)) i.e., infinite regress in originated conditions, whether successive or simultaneous, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a simplified proof for God's power. The author offers an alternative argument: if God were a necessitarian (mujib bi-dhat), then the effect (the world) would have to be eternal (qadim). Since the world is obviously originated (hadith), this contradicts the assumption. The entailment (mulazama) is explained: the effect of an eternal cause must be eternal because if it were originated, it would require an originated condition to avoid a delay in the effect from the complete cause—a delay that is impossible. But that condition would itself require another condition, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of conditions, which is impossible whether the conditions are successive or simultaneous. Thus, the effect cannot be originated, so it must be eternal, but the world is not eternal, so the assumption that God is a necessitarian is false, proving that He is powerful (qadir).

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
قدم الحادث
شرط حادث
تسلسل
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof only works if we assume the world is temporal.
Correction: The proof uses the observed fact that the world is originated (not eternal) as a premise, not a theoretical assumption.

Checkpoint
According to the simplified proof, what would necessarily follow if God were a necessitarian (mujib bi-dhat)?
A) The originated would be eternal.
B) The originated would be impossible.
C) There would be no originated things.
D) The originated would require a preponderant.

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji proves God's power by showing that a necessitarian view leads to an impossible infinite regress of conditions, contradicting the observed temporality of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is powerful and not constrained by necessity, how does that change the way you approach Him in your daily life? Do you rely on Him as a free Agent, or do you treat Him like a force that must respond?
?
The proof shows that your existence is not a random necessity but a deliberate act of God's power. How does this reality compel you to live with greater gratitude and purpose today?
?
Consider the infinite regress: if God were not powerful, your very being would require an endless chain of conditions. Since He is powerful, you exist by His direct will. What does this demand of you in terms of obedience and trust?

Proof that God Cannot Be a Necessary Agent
Al-Jurjani presents the alternative proof for God's power via necessary agency entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God cannot be a necessary agent, and why this establishes His power.

Core Idea
If God were a necessary agent, temporal events would be eternal—an impossibility.

Al-Jurjani presents an alternative argument for God's power by showing that the concept of a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat) leads to an absurd conclusion. The proof uses a conditional argument and the impossibility of infinite regress. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If...then...'
2
Identify the conditional premise
The passage starts with a conditional: if God were a necessary agent, then temporal events would be eternal. Identify this statement and its obvious falsehood.
The consequent (temporal events eternal) is clearly false, so the antecedent must also be false.
3
Understand the entailment explanation
Read the explanation of why the conditional holds: the effect of an eternal necessary agent must be eternal. If it were temporal, it would require a temporal condition to avoid non-occurrence, leading to infinite regress.
The regress argument shows why temporal events cannot be the effect of a necessary agent.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, the antecedent 'God is a necessary agent' is false. Therefore, God must be powerful (qadir) and acts by choice.
This proof does not rely on the temporality of the world; it is a direct logical entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شئت قلت : في إثبات كونه قادراً ( لو كان الباري تعالى موجباً بالذات لزم قدم الحادث والتالي باطل) بطلاناً ظاهراً ، (و) أما (بيان الملازمة ) فهو أن أثر الموجب القديم يجب أن يكون قديماً، إذ ( لو حدث لتوقف على شرط حادث) كيلا يلزم التخلف عن الموجب التام وذلك الشرط الحادث يتوقف أيضاً على شرط آخر حادث (و) حينئذ ( تسلسل ) أي لزم التسلسل في الشروط الحادثة متعاقبة أو مجتمعة وكلاهما محال .
And if you wish you may say: in proving that He is powerful: (If the Creator, exalted, were a necessary agent by essence, then the temporal event would be eternal, and the consequent is false) clearly false. (And) as for (the explanation of the entailment) it is that the effect of the eternal necessary agent must be eternal, since (if it occurred temporally it would depend on a temporal condition) so that non-occurrence would not follow from the complete necessary agent, and that temporal condition also depends on another temporal condition, (and) then (there would be an infinite regress) i.e., an infinite regress in temporal conditions, whether successive or simultaneous, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative formulation (wa in shi'ta qulta) of the proof that God is powerful (qadir) by refuting the notion that He is a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat). The proof is a conditional argument: if God were a necessary agent, then any temporal event (hadith) would have to be eternal (qadim), which is obviously false. The explanation of the entailment (bayān al-mulāzama) is given: the effect of an eternal necessary agent must be eternal; if it were temporal, it would require a temporal condition to avoid non-occurrence from the complete agent, and that condition would require another condition, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) in temporal conditions, whether successive or simultaneous—both of which are impossible. Thus, the denial of the consequent proves that God is not a necessary agent, but powerful.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
قدم الحادث
التخلف عن الموجب التام
تسلسل الشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this proof relies on the temporality of the world, but it is a direct logical entailment independent of that.
Correction: The proof does not assume the world is temporal; it only shows that if God were a necessary agent, then any temporal event would have to be eternal, which is false, so the premise must be false.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the conditional proof in this passage?
A) The world is eternal.
B) God has no power over temporal events.
C) God cannot be a necessary agent because that would make temporal events eternal.
D) Temporal events are impossible without an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The proof that God is powerful is established by showing that the concept of a necessary agent leads to an impossible infinite regress, so God must act through power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a necessary agent, your prayers would be mechanically answered or not based on pre-fixed conditions. How does the truth that He is powerful and acts by will affect the way you plead with Him in du'a, knowing He can choose to respond according to His wisdom?
?
The proof shows that an infinite chain of excuses (conditions) is impossible. In your own life, do you ever create an infinite regress of reasons to delay obeying Allah? How does realizing the impossibility of such a regress call you to break that cycle and turn directly to the Powerful One without further conditions?
